                        cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Single-crystal based studies for correlating the properties and high-voltage performance of Li[NixMnyCo1-x-y]O2 cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Jian; Chen, Guoying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5463-5474</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Safe and stable cycling of lithium-ion battery cathodes at high voltages is essential for meeting next-generation energy storage demands, yet the lack of fundamental understanding of the correlation of a material's properties and reactivities largely hinders current progress.  In the present study, we show how single-crystal samples with well-controlled phys. characteristics can be used to unambiguously establish the relationships among specific properties, surface chem. and electrochem. performance, enabling rational design of better performing cathode materials.  Layered Li[NixMnyCo1-x-y]O2 (NMC) crystals, with four different particle shapes of an octahedron (Oct), truncated octahedron (T-Oct), polyhedron (Poly) and platelet (Plate), were prepd. to vary the presence of (104), (001) and (012) family facets on the surface.  This represents the first exptl. verification of NMC morphologies that were theor. calcd. in the past.  Systematic studies on the impact of isolated phys. properties reveal the important roles of the Ni content, particle size and facet on surface stability and electrochem. performance.  We show that compared to (012) surface dominated samples, high-voltage cycling stability was much improved on the (001) dominated Plate sample, suggesting that replacing the (012) surface with lower energy (001) and/or (104) surfaces can be effective in stabilizing NMCs during high energy applications.  Our study further provides insights into how tailoring a material's surface properties can be used as an important route in balancing cathode capacity and stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.0c02302"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure and Interface Design Enable Stable Li-Rich Cathode</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>142</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">19</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">8918</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">8927</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/jacs.0c02302</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D142%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D8918-8927%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26issue%3D19%26author%3DC.%2BCui%26author%3DX.%2BFan%26author%3DX.%2BZhou%26author%3DJ.%2BChen%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BMa%26author%3DC.%2BYang%26author%3DE.%2BHu%26author%3DX.-Q.%2BYang%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26title%3DStructure%2Band%2BInterface%2BDesign%2BEnable%2BStable%2BLi-Rich%2BCathode%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fjacs.0c02302&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c07504&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fjacs.0c02302&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure and Interface Design Enable Stable Li-Rich Cathode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cui, Chunyu; Fan, Xiulin; Zhou, Xiuquan; Chen, Ji; Wang, Qinchao; Ma, Lu; Yang, Chongyin; Hu, Enyuan; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">142</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">19</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8918-8927</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-rich layered-oxide cathodes have the highest theor. energy d. among all the intercalated cathodes, which have attracted intense interests for high-energy Li-ion batteries.  However, O3-structured layered-oxide cathodes suffer from a low initial Coulombic efficiency (CE), severe voltage fade, and poor cycling stability because of the continuous oxygen release, structural rearrangements due to irreversible transition-metal migration, and serious side reactions between the delithiated cathode and electrolyte.  Herein, we report that these challenges are migrated by using a stable O2-structured Li1.2Ni0.13Co0.13Mn0.54O2 (O2-LR-NCM) and all-fluorinated electrolyte.  The O2-LR-NCM can restrict the transition metals migrating into the Li layer, and the in situ formed fluorinated cathode-electrolyte interphase (CEI) on the surface of the O2-LR-NCM from the decompn. of all-fluorinated electrolyte during initial cycles effectively restrains the structure transition, suppresses the O2 release, and thereby safeguards the transition metal redox couples, enabling a highly reversible and stable oxygen redox reaction.  O2-LR-NCM in all fluorinated electrolytes achieves a high initial CE of 99.82%, a cycling CE of &gt;99.9%, a high reversible capacity of 278 mAh/g, and high capacity retention of 83.3% after 100 cycles.  The synergic design of electrolyte and cathode structure represents a promising direction to stabilize high-energy cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref54" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202308257"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unveiling the Origin of Air Stability in Polyanion and Layered-Oxide Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries and Their Practical Application Considerations</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">XPS-Surface Analysis of SEI Layers on Li-Ion Cathodes: Part I. Investigation of Initial Surface Chemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schulz, Natalia; Hausbrand, Rene; Dimesso, Lucangelo; Jaegermann, Wolfram</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">165</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A819-A832</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this contribution, we investigate the initial surface chem. on fully lithiated LiCoO2 thin film model electrodes in the electrolyte solvent di-Et carbonate (DEC) and the LiPF6-electrolyte by means of soaking expts.  The interfacial layer compn. is analyzed by XPS, and possible layer morphologies and spontaneous formation mechanisms are discussed in detail.  Upon decompn. of DEC a layered system of surface-bound semi-org. components (inner layer) and cross-linked org. moieties (outer layer) is formed, while a change of the Co3+ oxidn. state and thus a surface corrosion of LiCoO2 was not obsd.  In contrast, the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film of the LiPF6-electrolyte soaked electrode showed an inner layer, contg. predominantly corroded LiCoO2, i.e. Co(II,III)xOy(OH)z and LiF as well as aliph. fluoroorg. species.  The outer SEI layer consists mainly of a poly-org. network and randomly distributed LixPOyFz domains.  The thickness of the deposit on the electrolyte soaked electrode surface was only half as thick due to the significantly lower amt. of org. and semi-org. compds.  Our investigation indicates that the solvent decompn. is related to the catalytically active LiCoO2 surface, which is passivated by reaction products such as LiF originating from HF induced processes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref58" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1002/chem.202303741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved Interface Construction on Anode and Cathode for Na-Ion Batteries Using Ultralow-Concentration Electrolyte Containing Dual-Additives</span>. <i>Chemistry</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2024</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">e202303741</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/chem.202303741</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D30%26publication_year%3D2024%26journal%3DChemistry%26author%3DY.%2BLin%26author%3DX.%2BJin%26author%3DS.%2BGao%26author%3DF.%2BLiu%26author%3DS.%2BHuang%26author%3DX.%2BYang%26author%3DY.%2BMeng%26title%3DImproved%2BInterface%2BConstruction%2Bon%2BAnode%2Band%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%2BUsing%2BUltralow-Concentration%2BElectrolyte%2BContaining%2BDual-Additives%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fchem.202303741&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c07504&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fchem.202303741&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

                10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b05269</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D30%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D1155-1163%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26issue%3D3%26author%3DH.-H.%2BRyu%26author%3DK.-J.%2BPark%26author%3DC.%2BS.%2BYoon%26author%3DY.-K.%2BSun%26title%3DCapacity%2BFading%2Bof%2BNi-Rich%2BLi%255BNixCoyMn1%25E2%2580%2593x%25E2%2580%2593y%255DO2%2B%25280.6%2B%25E2%2589%25A4%2Bx%2B%25E2%2589%25A4%2B0.95%2529%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BHigh-Energy-Density%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BBulk%2Bor%2BSurface%2BDegradation%253F%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.7b05269&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c07504&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.7b05269&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit59R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">59</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Capacity Fading of Ni-Rich Li[NixCoyMn1-x-y]O2 (0.6 ≤ x ≤ 0.95) Cathodes for High-Energy-Density Lithium-Ion Batteries: Bulk or Surface Degradation?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ryu, Hoon-Hee; Park, Kang-Joon; Yoon, Chong S.; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1155-1163</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ni-rich Li[NixCoyMn1-x-y]O2 cathodes (x = 0.6, 0.8, 0.9, and 0.95) were tested to characterize the capacity fading mechanism of extremely rich Ni compns.  Increasing the Ni fraction in the cathode delivered a higher discharge capacity (192.9 mA h g-1 for Li[Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2]O2 vs. 235.0 mA h g-1 for Li[Ni0.95Co0.025Mn0.025]O2); however, the cycling stability was substantially reduced.  Li[Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2]O2 and Li[Ni0.8Co0.1Mn0.1]O2 retained more than 95% of their resp. initial capacities after 100 cycles, while the capacity retention of Li[Ni0.9Co0.05Mn0.05]O2 and Li[Ni0.95Co0.025Mn0.025]O2 was limited to 85% during the same cycling period.  The relatively inferior cycling stability of Li[NixCoyMn1-x-y]O2 with x &gt; 0.8 is attributed to the phase transition near the charge-end, causing an abrupt anisotropic shrinkage (or expansion during discharge), which was suppressed for compns. of x &lt; 0.8.  Residual stress stemming from the phase transition destabilized the internal microcracks and allowed the microcracks to propagate to the surface, providing channels for electrolyte penetration and subsequent degrdn. of the exposed internal surfaces formed by the microcracks.  Further developments in particle morphol. are required to dissipate the intrinsic lattice strain, stabilize the surface, and modify the compn. to attain a satisfactory long-term cycling stability, and hence battery life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref60" class="refNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit60" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.02.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kinoshita, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hosokawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Morigaki, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakura, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Capacity fade of LiAl<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>1–x–y</sub>Co<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode for lithium-ion batteries during accelerated calendar and cycle life tests (surface analysis of LiAl<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>1–x–y</sub>Co<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode after cycle tests in restricted depth of discharge ranges)</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>258</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">210</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">217</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.02.018</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D258%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D210-217%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DS.%2BWatanabe%26author%3DM.%2BKinoshita%26author%3DT.%2BHosokawa%26author%3DK.%2BMorigaki%26author%3DK.%2BNakura%26title%3DCapacity%2Bfade%2Bof%2BLiAlyNi1%25E2%2580%2593x%25E2%2580%2593yCoxO2%2Bcathode%2Bfor%2Blithium-ion%2Bbatteries%2Bduring%2Baccelerated%2Bcalendar%2Band%2Bcycle%2Blife%2Btests%2B%2528surface%2Banalysis%2Bof%2BLiAlyNi1%25E2%2580%2593x%25E2%2580%2593yCoxO2%2Bcathode%2Bafter%2Bcycle%2Btests%2Bin%2Brestricted%2Bdepth%2Bof%2Bdischarge%2Branges%2529%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2014.02.018&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c07504&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2014.02.018&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Capacity fade of LiAlyNi1-x-yCoxO2 cathode for lithium-ion batteries during accelerated calendar and cycle life tests (surface analysis of LiAlyNi1-x-yCoxO2 cathode after cycle tests in restricted depth of discharge ranges)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Watanabe, Shoichiro; Kinoshita, Masahiro; Hosokawa, Takashi; Morigaki, Kenichi; Nakura, Kensuke</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">258</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">210-217</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cycle performance at 60° for a LiAl0.10Ni0.76Co0.14O2 (NCA) cathode/graphite cell was greatly improved when a DOD range in charge-discharge cycling (ΔDOD) was restricted.  The deterioration mechanism was analyzed by XPS, high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and scanning transmission electron microscopy-electron energy-loss spectroscopy (STEM-EELS).  Only after the cycle test in the ΔDOD of 0-100%, many micro-cracks were generated in the inter-surface between the primary particles which aggregated to form the secondary particles, and a NiO-like resistance layer with Fm3m rock salt structure was formed on each primary particle which was contact with other primary particles and electrolyte.  The lack of contact between the primary particles with the micro-crack generation and the formation of the new resistance layer are responsible for the capacity fading and the rise in impedance during charge-discharge cycle in the wide ΔDOD.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript"                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis Method for Long Cycle Life Lithium-Ion Cathode Material: Nickel-Rich Core-Shell LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Qi; Dang, Rongbin; Chen, Minmin; Lee, Yulin; Hu, Zhongbo; Xiao, Xiaoling</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-Ni materials with core-shell structures, whose bulk is rich in Ni content and the outer shell is rich in Mn content, have been demonstrated to improve cycle stability.  The high-Ni cathode material LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2 is a very promising material for Li-ion batteries; however, its low rate performance and esp. cycle performance currently hamper further commercialization.  This study presents a new synthesis method to prep. this core-shell material (LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2@x[Li-Mn-O], x = 0.01, 0.03, 0.06).  Electrochem. data show that LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2@x[Li-Mn-O] (x = 0.03, CS-0.03) exhibits the best high-rate performance, cycle stability, and thermal stability.  The initial discharge capacity of the core-shell sample CS-0.03 is 118 mAh g-1, which is almost the same as the discharge capacity of pristine LiNi0.8Mn0.1Co0.1O2 (117 mAh g-1) at the rate of 10 C in the voltage range of 3.0-4.3 V.  Notably the capacity decay of CS-0.03 is 18.4% after 200 cycles compared to 27% decay in capacity of the pristine sample.  Also, CS-0.03 exhibits better thermal cycling stability.  The capacity retention of the CS-0.03 sample reached 65.1% which is over 1.3 times than that of the pristine 1, whose capacity retention is 49.2% after 105 cycles (55°).  Evidently, the core-shell structured CS-0.03 sample has excellent cycle stability and this synthesis method can be applied to other cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01598b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Etacheri, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Marom, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Elazari, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Salitra, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges in the development of advanced Li-ion batteries: a review</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Li-ion battery technol. has become very important in recent years as these batteries show great promise as power sources that can lead us to the elec. vehicle (EV) revolution.  The development of new materials for Li-ion batteries is the focus of research in prominent groups in the field of materials science throughout the world.  Li-ion batteries can be considered to be the most impressive success story of modern electrochem. in the last two decades.  They power most of today's portable devices, and seem to overcome the psychol. barriers against the use of such high energy d. devices on a larger scale for more demanding applications, such as EV.  Since this field is advancing rapidly and attracting an increasing no. of researchers, it is important to provide current and timely updates of this constantly changing technol.  In this review, we describe the key aspects of Li-ion batteries: the basic science behind their operation, the most relevant components, anodes, cathodes, electrolyte solns., as well as important future directions for R&amp;D of advanced Li-ion batteries for demanding use, such as EV and load-leveling applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c25262"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Roy, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banerjee, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogale, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Search for new anode materials for high performance Li-ion batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to an unmatched combination of power and energy d. along with cyclic stability, the Li-ion battery has qualified itself to be the highest performing rechargeable battery.  Taking both transportable and stationary energy storage requirements into consideration, Li-ion batteries indeed stand tall in comparison to any other existing rechargeable battery technologies.  However, graphite, which is still one of the best performing Li-ion anodes, has specific drawbacks in fulfilling the ever-increasing energy and power d. requirements of the modern world.  Therefore, further research on alternative anode materials is absolutely essential.  Equally important is the search for and enhanced use of right earth abundant materials for battery electrodes so as to bring down the costs of the battery systems.  In this spotlight article, we discuss the current research progress in the area of alternative anode materials for Li-ion battery, putting our own research work over the past several years into perspective.  Starting from conversion anode systems like oxides and sulfides, to insertion cum alloying systems like transition metal carbides, to molecularly engineered open framework systems like metal org. frameworks (MOFs), covalent org. frameworks (COFs), and org.-inorg. hybrid perovskites (OIHPs), this spotlight provides a complete essence of the recent developments in the area of alternative anodes.  The possible and potential impact of these new anode materials is detailed and discussed here.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA07392A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Belharouak, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A comprehensive study of the role of transition metals in O3-type layered Na[Ni<sub>x</sub>Co<sub>y</sub>Mn<sub>z</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (x = 1/3, 0.5, 0.6, and 0.8) cathodes for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium layered oxides show great promise as affordable alternatives to lithium layered oxides, but their poor cycle life and air stability limit their practical potential.  Micron-scale single crystals with greater packing d. and lower surface area can overcome these challenges and improve performance compared to the traditional polycryst. morphol.  Herein, the authors present the synthesis of layered O3-type Na(Ni0.3Fe0.4Mn0.3)O2 single-crystals with greatly improved cycle life and air stability.  A molten-salt synthesis technique is adopted with excess sodium hydroxide to obtain platelet-like single crystals.  Because the main mechanisms of both capacity fade and air degrdn. occur as a result of surface reactions at the opening of the sodium diffusion channels, particle morphol. is found to be a crit. metric for materials performance.  More important than particle size or total surface area, the smaller proportion of exposed edge planes in the platelet morphol. greatly reduces the amt. of harmful surface reactions.  Furthermore, the molten-salt method is found to eliminate the need for copptd. precursors and even form better morphol., starting from metal oxides instead of copptd. hydroxides.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2018.07.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A phase-transition-free cathode for sodium-ion batteries with ultralong cycle life</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>52</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">88</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">94</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Sodium-ion-battery, using the layered oxide material as cathodes coupled with carbon anodes, has been proposed for utilization in practice long time ago. This review focuses on the latest progress in both cathode and anode materials for SIBs.  It also details research in binders and additives and their effects on the SIB system.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium-ion battery cathode, O3-type NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2, has been systematically investigated by synchrotron-based analyses to characterize the structural behavior during electrochem. reaction.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy shows reversible redox process upon cycling and clearly proves that both Ni and Fe are active in Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 and that redox couples of Ni2+/Ni4+via Ni3+ and Fe3+/Fe4+ are responsible for charge compensation.  Specifically, the capacity is mainly realized with Ni2+/Ni4+ and slightly from Fe3+/Fe4+ under charging voltage of 4.0 V.  At high voltage (&gt;4.0 V), however, Fe redox reaction is dominant and Ni contributes slightly to capacity.  In structural evolution, Na1-xNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 undergoes phase transformation from O3 to P3 phase below 4.0 V and further reaches OP2 structure above 4.0 V along with a significant contraction of d-spacing.  Moreover, quant. anal. of extended X-ray absorption fine structure suggests that disorder of local structure for Fe is greatly increased in high voltage region.  Accordingly, collapse of d-spacing can be considered as being caused by Fe migration in the TM layer into the neighboring Na layer.  This study thus provides detailed redox behavior and factor of structural distortions under high voltage region by considering bulk and local structural changes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201702024"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">By increasing the charging voltage, a cell specific energy of &gt;400 W h kg-1 is achievable with LiNi0.8Mn0.1Co0.1O2 in Li metal batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Complex chemomech. interplay exists over a wide range of length scales within the hierarchically structured lithium-ion battery.  At the mesoscale, the interdependent structural complexity and chem. heterogeneity collectively govern the local chem. and, as a result, critically affect the cell level performance.  Here the morphol. and state are investigated of charge (SOC) inhomogeneity within secondary NCA particles that were cycled in solid polymer batteries.  Substantial inhomogeneity is obsd. in the nickel oxidn. state (a proxy for SOC) and loss of structural integrity within secondary particles after only 20 cycles due to significant intergranular cracking.  The formation of mesoscale cracks causes loss of ionic and elec. contact within cathode particles, triggering increases in local impedance and rearrangement of transport pathways for charge carriers.  This can eventually lead to deactivation of subparticle level domains in solid-state lithium-ion batteries.  The findings highlight the importance of proper mesoscale strain and defect management in polymer lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202400569"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kumar, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ming, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphic, Quantitation, Visualization, Standardization, Digitization, and Intelligence of Electrolyte and Electrolyte-Electrode Interface</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In the past two decades, exploring new electrolytes for SIBs has generally relied on the "solid electrolyte interphase (SEI)" theory to optimize the electrolyte components.  However, many obsd. phenomena cannot be fully explained by the SEI theory.  Therefore, electrolyte solvation structure and electrode-electrolyte interface behavior have recently received tremendous research interest to explain the improved performance.  Considering there is currently no review paper focusing on the solvation structure of electrolytes in SIBs, a systematic survey on SIBs is provided, in which the specific solvation structure design guidelines and their consequent impact on the electrochem. performance are elucidated.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Two major strategies are currently pursued to improve the energy d. of Li-ion batteries using LiNixCoyMnzO2 (NCM) cathode materials.  One is to increase the fraction of redox active Ni (≥80%), which allows larger amts. of Li to be extd. at a given cutoff voltage (Umax).  The other is to increase Umax, in particular for medium-Ni content NCM materials.  However, the accompanying lattice changes ultimately lead to capacity fading in both cases.  Here the structural changes occurring in Li1.02NixCoyMnzO2 (with x = 1/3, 0.5, 0.6, 0.7, 0.8 and 0.85) during cycling operation in the voltage range between 3.0 and 4.6 V vs. Li are quantified by operando x-ray diffraction combined with detailed Rietveld anal.  All samples show a large decrease in unit cell vol. upon charging, ranging from 2.4% for NCM111 (33% Ni) to 8.0% for NCM851005 (85% Ni).  To make a fair comparison of the structural stability of the different NCM materials, energy densities as a function of Umax are estd. and correlated with x-ray diffraction results.  NCMs with a lower Ni content allow for specific energies similar to that of, e.g., Ni-rich NCM811 (80% Ni) when operated at sufficiently high Umax, but still undergo less pronounced changes in structure.  Nevertheless, as indicated by charge/discharge tests, the capacity retention of low- and medium-Ni content NCMs cycled to high Umax is also strongly affected by factors other than stability of the layered crystal lattice (electrolyte decompn. etc.).  Overall, the complexity of the degrdn. processes needs to be better understood to identify optimal cycling conditions for specific cathode compns.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06701"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, G.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guiming, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">McDonald, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type sodium nickel manganese oxide-based cathode materials with higher energy densities are prime candidates for applications in rechargeable sodium ion batteries.  A systematic study combining in situ high energy X-ray diffraction (HEXRD), ex situ X-ray absorption fine spectroscopy (XAFS), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and solid-state NMR (SS-NMR) techniques was carried out to gain a deep insight into the structural evolution of P2-Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07) during cycling.  In situ HEXRD and ex situ TEM measurements indicate that an irreversible phase transition occurs upon sodium insertion-extn. of Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.67O2.  Zinc doping of this system results in a high structural reversibility.  XAFS measurements indicate that both materials are almost completely dependent on the Ni4+/Ni3+/Ni2+ redox couple to provide charge/discharge capacity.  SS-NMR measurements indicate that both reversible and irreversible migration of transition metal ions into the sodium layer occurs in the material at the fully charged state.  The irreversible migration of transition metal ions triggers a structural distortion, leading to the obsd. capacity and voltage fading.  Our results allow a new understanding of the importance of improving the stability of transition metal layers.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2019.03.031"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, T.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bae, I.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ti-doped O3-type Na[Tix(Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2)1-x]O2 is studied as a high-performance cathode material for practical sodium-ion batteries.  The effect of partial Ti doping on the structural and electrochem. properties of O3-type Na[Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2]O2 materials is investigated by varying the Ti content (x) in Na[Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2]O2 from 0 to 0.01 to 0.03.  SEM images show that upon doping, the primary particles aggregate and form densely packed secondary particles, yielding enhanced mech. strength and high tap d. of ∼2.3 g cm-3.  The compact morphol. of the particles effectively minimizes the void vol. for possible electrolyte penetration that usually leads to unwanted side reactions.  In addn., partial doping of Ti in the transition-metal layer greatly improve the structural stability.  By taking both morphol. and structural advantages, the O3-type Na[Ti0.03(Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2)0.97]O2 cathode demonstrate the great enhancements of battery performances in terms of capacity, cycle retention, rate capability, and thermal properties.  Pouch-type full cells assembled by combining the present cathodes with hard carbon anodes show good practical applicability, with an outstanding cycle retention of 77% over 400 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Single-cryst. layered cathodes are often desirable for advanced lithium-ion batteries.  However, constrained by the accessible temp. range to prevent lithium evapn., lattice defects and particle agglomerations, the prodn. of single-cryst. cathodes with high phase purity, good electrochem. performance and scalability remains challenging.  Here we invent a new mechanochem. activation process that offers a general soln. to the conundrum of synthesizing coarse single-crystal cathodes with Li-/Mn-rich or Ni-rich chem., which differs from the equipment- and energy-intense and long-duration mechanochem. routes that are difficult to scale up. It efficiently deagglomerates the polycryst. precursors, repacks the crystals and homogenizes the lithium-salt distribution, thus enabling facile particle coarsening later into the single-cryst. morphol. with improved electrochem. performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2022.139431"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Design and mechanism exploration of single-crystalline NCM811 materials with superior comprehensive performance for Li-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Although single-cryst. LiNixCoyMn1-x-yO2 (SC NCM) materials have been confirmed to have advantages in cycle performance, storage performance and thermal stability, the comprehensive performance of SC NCM materials is not perfect enough nowadays.  And the current research on the modification mostly focuses on polycryst. NCM materials, the modification of SC NCM materials is rare.  Therefore, the synthesis and modification of SC NCM materials deserve special attention.  In this work, the SC high-nickel LiNi0.8Co0.1Mn0.1O2 (NCM811) is modified by doping with titanium (Ti) element. Doping enlarges the transport channel of Li+ in the material, thereby increasing the rate property.  The lattice slip phenomenon of the modified material under high voltage is significantly suppressed, and the oxygen release and the reactivity with the electrolyte at high temp. of the material after delithiation are reduced, so the high voltage resistance and thermal stability are significantly enhanced.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The irreversible consumption of sodium at the anode side during the first cycle prominently reduces the energy d. of Na-ion batteries.  Different sacrificial cathode additives have been recently reported to address this problem; however, crit. issues such as byproducts (e.g., CO2) release during cycling and incompatibility with current battery fabrication procedures potentially deteriorate the full-cell performance and prevent the practical application.  Herein, an additive-free self-presodiation strategy is proposed to create lattice-coherent but component-dependent O3-NaxTMMnO2 (TM = transition metal ion(s)) cathodes by a quenching treatment rather than the general natural cooling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The ever-increasing energy d. requirements in elec. vehicles (EVs) have boosted the development of Ni-rich layered oxide cathodes for state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries.  Nevertheless, the commercialization of polycryst. Ni-rich cathodes (PCNCs) is hindered by the severe performance degrdn. and safety concerns that are tightly related to its particle cracking during cycling.  Single-cryst. Ni-rich cathodes (SCNCs) with eliminated grain boundaries and high mech. strength have recently attracted extensive attention owing to their superior structural and cycling stability, which present high crack resistance during electrochem. operation.  Various articles have focused on the trial-and-error synthesis and modifications of SCNCs, as well as the comparison of performances and mechanisms with PCNCs.  However, there has been much less effort in systematic anal. and summary to reveal their key challenges, controversies, and the corresponding primary causes.  In this review, the advantages and debates in structural and electrochem. properties of SCNCs over PCNCs are summarized to provide fundamental understanding of SCNCs.  Then the current practical issues and challenges are comprehensively discussed from the viewpoints of both academia and industry, as well as the proposed modification strategies and underlying mechanisms for SCNCs.  The outlook and perspectives are further given to facilitate the com. applications of SCNCs in high-performance EVs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.138982"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Truong, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study we investigated the effect of the Li-excess on electrochem. properties of LixNi0.6Co0.2Mn0.2O2 cathode materials, which was obtained by sintering Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2(OH)2 with a various amts. of LiOH (samples with Li-excesses of 5, 10, 20, and 25 mol% are denoted herein as NCM-5, NCM-10, NCM-20, and NCM-25, resp.). When the Li-excess increased, Rietveld refinement revealed that cation mixing occurred, the lattice parameters decreased, the transition metal slab thickness increased, and the inter-slab Li space thickness decreased.  Nevertheless, an appropriate degree of cation mixing could retain the structural stability and improve the rate capability of the electrodes.  It was found that the sample contg. the 20 mol% Li-excess (NCM-20) achieved the best cyclic stability, with a capacity retention of 90% at a current rate of 1C/1C for 200 cycles between 2.8 and 4.5 V at room temp.  In situ X-ray diffraction confirmed the greater stability of the crystal phase and phys. structure of NCM-20 upon initial cycling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To facilitate the practical realization of sodium-ion batteries, the energy d., detd. by the output operating voltage and/or capacity, needs to be improved to the level of com. Li-ion batteries.  Herein, O3-type Na0.98Ca0.01[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 is synthesized by incorporating Ca2+ into the NaO6 octahedron of Na[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 and its potential use as a cathode material for high energy d. Within a wide operating voltage range of 2.0-4.3 V, O3-type Na0.98Ca0.01[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 exhibits a reversible O3-P3-O3 phase transition with small vol. changes compared to Ca-free Na[Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 because of the strong interaction between Ca2+ and O2- and delivers a high reversible capacity of 209 mA h g-1 at 15 mA g-1 with improved cycling stability.  Moreover, Ca substitution improves the practically useful aspects such as thermal and air stability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Safe and stable cycling of lithium-ion battery cathodes at high voltages is essential for meeting next-generation energy storage demands, yet the lack of fundamental understanding of the correlation of a material's properties and reactivities largely hinders current progress.  In the present study, we show how single-crystal samples with well-controlled phys. characteristics can be used to unambiguously establish the relationships among specific properties, surface chem. and electrochem. performance, enabling rational design of better performing cathode materials.  Layered Li[NixMnyCo1-x-y]O2 (NMC) crystals, with four different particle shapes of an octahedron (Oct), truncated octahedron (T-Oct), polyhedron (Poly) and platelet (Plate), were prepd. to vary the presence of (104), (001) and (012) family facets on the surface.  This represents the first exptl. verification of NMC morphologies that were theor. calcd. in the past.  Systematic studies on the impact of isolated phys. properties reveal the important roles of the Ni content, particle size and facet on surface stability and electrochem. performance.  We show that compared to (012) surface dominated samples, high-voltage cycling stability was much improved on the (001) dominated Plate sample, suggesting that replacing the (012) surface with lower energy (001) and/or (104) surfaces can be effective in stabilizing NMCs during high energy applications.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-rich layered-oxide cathodes have the highest theor. energy d. among all the intercalated cathodes, which have attracted intense interests for high-energy Li-ion batteries.  However, O3-structured layered-oxide cathodes suffer from a low initial Coulombic efficiency (CE), severe voltage fade, and poor cycling stability because of the continuous oxygen release, structural rearrangements due to irreversible transition-metal migration, and serious side reactions between the delithiated cathode and electrolyte.  Herein, we report that these challenges are migrated by using a stable O2-structured Li1.2Ni0.13Co0.13Mn0.54O2 (O2-LR-NCM) and all-fluorinated electrolyte. O2-LR-NCM in all fluorinated electrolytes achieves a high initial CE of 99.82%, a cycling CE of &gt;99.9%, a high reversible capacity of 278 mAh/g, and high capacity retention of 83.3% after 100 cycles.  The synergic design of electrolyte and cathode structure represents a promising direction to stabilize high-energy cathodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202308257"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unveiling the Origin of Air Stability in Polyanion and Layered-Oxide Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries and Their Practical Application Considerations</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ni-rich Li[NixCoyMn1-x-y]O2 cathodes (x = 0.6, 0.8, 0.9, and 0.95) were tested to characterize the capacity fading mechanism of extremely rich Ni compns.  Increasing the Ni fraction in the cathode delivered a higher discharge capacity (192.9 mA h g-1 for Li[Ni0.6Co0.2Mn0.2]O2 vs. 235.0 mA h g-1 for Li[Ni0.95Co0.025Mn0.025]O2); however, the cycling stability was substantially reduced. The relatively inferior cycling stability of Li[NixCoyMn1-x-y]O2 with x &gt; 0.8 is attributed to the phase transition near the charge-end, causing an abrupt anisotropic shrinkage (or expansion during discharge), which was suppressed for compns. of x &lt; 0.8.  Residual stress stemming from the phase transition destabilized the internal microcracks and allowed the microcracks to propagate to the surface, providing channels for electrolyte penetration and subsequent degrdn. of the exposed internal surfaces formed by the microcracks.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cycle performance at 60° for a LiAl0.10Ni0.76Co0.14O2 (NCA) cathode/graphite cell was greatly improved when a DOD range in charge-discharge cycling (ΔDOD) was restricted.  The deterioration mechanism was analyzed by XPS, high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and scanning transmission electron microscopy-electron energy-loss spectroscopy (STEM-EELS).  Only after the cycle test in the ΔDOD of 0-100%, many micro-cracks were generated in the inter-surface between the primary particles which aggregated to form the secondary particles, and a NiO-like resistance layer with Fm3m rock salt structure was formed on each primary particle which was contact with other primary particles and electrolyte.  The lack of contact between the primary particles with the micro-crack generation and the formation of the new resistance layer are responsible for the capacity fading and the rise in impedance during charge-discharge cycle in the wide ΔDOD.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i11">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c07504?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c07504</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern of PC-NCM particles after natural cooling; XRD patterns of the cathode samples synthesized using different sodium contents (NaOH = 125, 130, 135, and 140%); XRD patterns of the SC-NCM cathode washed with DI water and ethanol; Rietveld refinement results of the SC-NCM and PC-NCM cathodes; SEM images of the SC-NCM and PC-NCM cathodes; EDX mapping images of the PC-NCM cathode; chemical composition of the SC-NCM and PC-NCM cathodes; and pore size distribution curves of the SC-NCM and PC-NCM cathodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c07504/suppl_file/am4c07504_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c07504/suppl_file/am4c07504_si_001.pdf">am4c07504_si_001.pdf (487.22 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="931884b36abd54b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="931884b36abd54b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="931884b36abd54b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];

        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="931884b36abd54b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="931884b36abd54b8-SJC">
$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
